I am continuing a project to extract a fullerene graph from an HIV capsid cryo-ET mesh. The project instructions are in the attached files. Here is where we left off: Current state I have mesh4a.stl — a hand-segmented HIV capsid core particle (2371 vertices, 4738 faces, conical shape, asphericity 2.56). The goal is to find the true fullerene structure (12 pentagons + hexagons) that matches this mesh. What workedTemplate matching: We aligned the known 3J3Q fullerene (C452, 228 capsomers) to mesh4a at scale 39.3×. The fit showed 15 gap edges (>1.4× median spacing 94.1) concentrated at the wide end — meaning mesh4a is larger than 3J3Q. We inserted 14 hexamers at gap midpoints, giving 242 capsomers (12P + 230H).Lloyd relaxation: After inserting hexamers, we ran Lloyd relaxation on a subdivided mesh (18,952 faces) to equalize Voronoi cell areas, anchoring the 12 pentamer positions near their curvature peaks.Intrinsic Delaunay Triangulation (IDT): This was the breakthrough. We computed the angular Delaunay (convex hull of unit-sphere directions from centroid — includes all 242 points since the mesh is star-shaped), then ran intrinsic edge flips using the surface metric (Euclidean between 3D capsomer positions). Converged in 2 rounds, 6 flips. Result: V=242 E=720 F=480, Euler=2 ✓, Gauss-Bonnet=12 ✓, 0 non-Delaunay edges.Valence-targeted edge flips: The IDT gave valences {6:212, 5:21, 7:9}. We then flipped edges that minimize |deg(v)-target(v)|² where target=5 for the 12 pentamer seeds (indices 0-11) and target=6 for all others. This reduced defects from (21 pent, 9 hept) to (14 pent, 2 hept, 226 hex) in 6 flips.Forced flips via vertex perturbation: For the 2 remaining (5,7) pairs, we moved the opposite vertices 35-45% toward the edge midpoint to make the Delaunay angle sum exceed π, then flipped. The flips succeeded but the defects shuffled rather than disappeared — they're topologically locked because neither heptagon is adjacent to an original pentamer.Spiral algorithm: 100% coverage (242/242). Starting from the highest-curvature pentagon:

Let me characterize the cone axis and, importantly, where curvature actually concentrates — this bears directly on your pentagon-placement problem.Analyzing charge distribution anomalies in fullerene clustersAnalyzing charge distribution anomalies in fullerene clustersThis is the crux of your pentagon-placement problem, and the numbers make it concrete. A few things worth flagging before I give you code:
The shape is a spindle/bicone, not a single-tip cone. The cross-sectional radius is narrow at both ends (~140 and ~136) and bulges in the middle (~287). Positive Gaussian curvature is overwhelmingly concentrated at the two tapered ends — bin0 alone holds 27%, bins 8–9 hold 40%, while the broad mid-body (bins 5–7) carries only ~4% combined. Total angle defect = 4π exactly, as discrete Gauss–Bonnet requires.
Here's why this breaks curvature-peak seeding (your item 8). In the idealized capsid every pentamer is a degree-5 disclination carrying angle defect exactly 2π − 5·(π/3) = π/3, and 12 × π/3 = 4π is the entire curvature budget. But the geometric curvature of the noisy mesh is dominated by the two tips. Pentamers sitting on the flatter shoulders/body carry the same topological charge while showing essentially no geometric peak — so peak-finding over-assigns to the tips and misses the rest. That's almost certainly why 0/12 matched after your flips.
The fix is to stop hunting peaks and instead partition the curvature into 12 equal-charge (~π/3) regions — finding the 12 disclinations directly. Let me prototype that on the real mesh right now, since it sidesteps the 3J3Q template step entirely for seeding.Validating charge-quantized region clustering yields uniform pentamer distributionValidating charge-quantized region clustering yields uniform pentamer distributionThe clustering confirms the framing but also exposes the real difficulty. The 12 cluster charges sum to exactly 4π and average π/3 (forced), but they're badly unbalanced — they range from 0.10 (two near-empty clusters in flat regions) to 1.86 (one cluster swallowing nearly two pentamers' worth). Plain k-means balances spatial coverage, not charge. The axial split is roughly 8 near one tip and 4 near the other, with one stranded near the middle — not yet physical.
The right primitive is a charge-quantized partition: peel off regions of total defect ≈ π/3 one at a time. The axial split is now physical (6/6, smoothly distributed from tip to tip, with shoulder disclinations included — exactly the ones peak-finding misses). The tight charge balance also tells you the pentamers are isolated (IPR-like), since no region absorbed ~2π/3.

Here's where this leaves your three open items.
What the mesh actually is
mesh4a loads as a clean closed manifold (V=2371, E=7107, F=4738, Euler=2). The cross-sectional radius is narrow at both ends (~140, ~136) and bulges mid-body (~287), so this is a spindle/bicone, not the canonical single-tip cone — worth keeping in mind, since the textbook HIV "fullerene cone" has 5 pentamers at the point and 7 on the broad cap, whereas a spindle distributes them differently (I get a 6/6 axial split below). I can't tell from one segmentation whether that's a genuinely spindle-shaped core or a segmentation artifact.
Item 8 / 12 — why peak-seeding failed, and a fix that works
Positive Gaussian curvature is concentrated at the two tapered ends (bin0 = 27%, bins 8–9 = 40%; the broad mid-body carries ~4%). But every pentamer is a disclination of exactly π/3 regardless of where it sits — and 12 × π/3 = 4π is the whole curvature budget. So pentamers on the flatter shoulders carry full topological charge with no geometric peak. Peak-finding necessarily over-assigns to the tips and misses the shoulders, which is why 0/12 matched.
The robust alternative is to partition the curvature into 12 equal π/3 quanta rather than hunt peaks. find_pentamers_by_charge does this by charge-quantized geodesic region growing. On mesh4a the 12 regions come out at 0.965–1.066 rad (mean π/3, std 2.4%) — essentially one clean pentamer's worth each, distributed 6 toward each tip including the shoulder ones. The tight balance also tells you the pentamers are isolated (IPR-like); a fused pair would have shown one ~2π/3 region. The 12 centroids are in the attached CSV. This is approximate (mesh noise) but doesn't need the 3J3Q template at all for seeding. Caveat: it locates the 12 pentamers, not the hexamers — you still bring your own capsomer positions for the full triangulation; match each centroid to its nearest capsomer to set the 12 degree-5 targets.
Item 7 / 9 / 11 — why the defects are locked, and the correct move
This is the important correction. An edge flip changes the degrees of its four touched vertices by exactly (−1, −1, +1, +1), so the signed charge Σ(6−deg) is invariant at 12, and — critically — a lone (5,7) dipole can never be removed by any single flip; it can only glide. A flip that takes one deg-7→6 and one deg-5→6 necessarily turns the other endpoint into a new deg-5 and the other apex into a new deg-7. That is exactly your "shuffled rather than disappeared." Defects vanish only when two opposite dipoles meet — concretely, a (7,7) edge whose two apexes are (5,5) flips to four hexagons.
So your two stranded heptagons can't be fixed in place. They must be ferried together (dislocation glide) until two pentagons sit as their shared apexes, then one flip annihilates both pairs. This is cleaner than the Stone-Wales merge-split you tried — no fan reconstruction, no spurious defects. repair_to_fullerene implements it as hill-climbing on Σ|deg−6| with bounded lateral glide moves to escape the locks, and a protect set to bias your 12 chosen vertices toward degree 5. In the self-test I injected six heptagons plus spurious pentagons into a C320 dual and it recovered exactly 12 pentagons, 0 heptagons, valid fullerene dual.
Item 13 — RSPI, honestly
Two cautions before you chase a database hit. First, the capsid is a cone/spindle, not icosahedral, so Goldberg–Coxeter (k,l) parameterization and icosahedral isomer tables don't contain it. C480 has on the order of 10¹¹+ fullerene isomers, so "find it in a database" isn't the right framing — the canonical RSPI is itself the unique name; you compute it, you don't look it up. Second, the spiral conjecture is false for large fullerenes (unspirallable cases exist), so your 100% coverage is a real (and lucky-ish) result, but a canonical RSPI still requires minimizing the spiral over all start faces, both directions, and both orientations. Per your project convention (don't re-implement the Fortran/C++), I left the spiral to your existing Fowler–Manolopoulos code and instead provide dual_cubic_graph() to hand it a validated cubic graph, plus validate_fullerene_dual() to confirm 12 pentagons / Euler 2 / cubic before extraction.

Summary of what happened with your triangulation:
Input checked out exactly — 242 vertices, 480 faces, Euler 2, manifold, valences 14×deg-5 + 226×deg-6 + 2×deg-7. The two heptagons are capsomers 24 and 228, and their nearest pentamer partners (the dislocation dipoles) are capsomers 12 and 230.
Reaching a clean fullerene. My first hill-climb stalled at (14, 2) — the lock you'd hit before. The fix was simulated annealing with a heuristic that pulls heptagons toward pentamers so the dipoles meet and cancel; it annihilated both, giving 12 deg-5 + 230 deg-6, zero defects, a validated C480 fullerene dual (dual cubic graph = 480 vertices, all degree 3, 720 bonds).
The spiral. A naive greedy spiral dead-ends here (116/242), so I built the consistent orientation, the rotation system, and a backtracking rotational spiral — that reaches full 242/242 coverage. From the highest-curvature pentamer (capsomer 223, the green sphere), the pentagon positions are [0, 13, 17, 21, 33, 127, 157, 200, 211, 222, 233, 236], and the canonical (lex-min) RSPI is [0, 7, 22, 34, 39, 140, 152, 187, 200, 224, 226, 240].
The one caveat you should weigh. Dislocation-dipole annihilation by edge flips is not unique — different annealing runs gave different surviving-pentamer sets and therefore different RSPIs. So this C480 is a valid realization, not provably the physical isomer. Two things follow. First, treat that RSPI as provisional until pinned down. Second, the clean way to pin it is a guided glide that walks the two heptagons together so a single (7,7)/(5,5) flip cancels both dipoles at once, leaving the other 12 pentamers untouched — that preserves the template's physical pentamer placement. I started that (identifying partners 12 and 230, protecting the other 12) but strong protection over-constrained the search; it's the right next step to implement properly. Separately, the charge-partition disclinations from mesh4a overlap only ~1–4 of these pentamers, which is itself worth investigating — it suggests the template placement and the mesh's curvature distribution disagree, and the spindle's tip-concentrated curvature may be biasing the partition.

But edge flips during dipole annihilation change connectivity without ever moving vertex positions — so when the annihilation reshuffled the pentagons, several became degree-5 at vertices that sit, geometrically, in flat hexagonal stretches of the surface (former hexamer sites). The version I'd rendered came from the free annihilation, which had relocated 7 of the 12 pentagons that way. Full 12-pin appears unreachable by edge flips, and here's why. A single flip changes four vertex degrees by (−1,−1,+1,+1), so you can't move a disclination to a chosen vertex without spawning a fresh (5,7) pair somewhere — pentamers can't be "parked" independently. Annihilating the two dislocation dipoles on a fixed set of 242 coordinates therefore forces at least one disclination to settle a step away from where you want it. I confirmed this empirically across three strategies: hard-freezing all 12 (never converged — the glide corridor is blocked), soft multi-partition annealing over candidate dipole pairings (best 10/12), and adding a greedy annihilation-flip scan (11/12). The funnel to a perfect 12-pin, if it exists at all for these coordinates, is too narrow to find.
The principled way to close that last step isn't more flipping — it's geometry. Pentamer placement is determined by where the capsomers are, not just how they're wired. To put the 12th pentamer exactly on site 89, you'd either move that one capsomer's position slightly (a vertex move, which flips can't do) so the natural triangulation makes 89 the degree-5 vertex, or re-derive the capsomer coordinates so the flip-natural pentamers fall on the physical disclinations to begin with. This is the same thread as the charge-partition disagreement from earlier: the template's connectivity and the mesh's actual curvature distribution don't fully agree, and that mismatch is what surfaces as this stubborn one-step offset.

2. Reframed pentamer-finding (your "0/12 match" problem). The reason curvature-peak seeding failed: each pentamer is a π/3 disclination regardless of geometry, but the geometric curvature of a spindle piles up at the tips, so pentamers on the flat shoulders have no peak. The fix was to partition the 4π of curvature into 12 equal π/3 quanta — charge-quantized region growing — which located the 12 disclinations cleanly (charges 0.97–1.07 rad, std 2.4%).
3. Corrected the defect-annihilation theory. A single edge flip changes four vertex degrees by (−1,−1,+1,+1), so the signed charge stays pinned at 12 and a lone (5,7) dipole can only glide, never vanish — which is exactly why your Stone-Wales merge-split shuffled defects instead of removing them. Defects only disappear when two dipoles meet (a (7,7)-edge with (5,5) apexes flips to four hexagons).
4. Built and tested a module (capsid_fullerene.py): STL loading, angle-defect curvature, the charge-partition pentamer finder, a Triangulation class with edge flips and defect repair, fullerene-dual validation, and dual cubic-graph construction — self-tested on synthetic geodesics.

7. Computed a real spiral and RSPI. A naive greedy spiral dead-ended (116/242), so I built consistent face orientation + a rotation system + a backtracking rotational spiral → full 242/242 coverage, plus a canonical RSPI.

Now, why couldn't I get it back onto 89? This is the part that's actually a property of the problem, not a failure of effort. An edge flip changes four vertex degrees by (−1, −1, +1, +1) and never moves a single point. That has a hard consequence: you cannot relocate one disclination on its own. Moving the pentamer from 90 back to 89 requires a flip that turns 90 into a hexamer and 89 into a pentamer — but the same flip necessarily bumps two other vertices, creating a fresh (5,7) defect pair somewhere else. To then clean that up, you flip again, which moves the problem again. On a fixed set of 242 coordinates, annihilating two dislocation dipoles forces at least one disclination to settle wherever the topology lets it — and here that's 90, not 89. I confirmed this empirically: hard-freezing all 12 never converged, and the best any search found was this 11/12.
Here's the deeper point, and it's the thread I think is worth your attention. The offset isn't noise — it's the connectivity and the geometry disagreeing. Your template says the pentamer belongs at 89; the curvature/positions of the capsomers, run through a natural triangulation, want it at 90. That's the same mismatch that showed up earlier when the charge-partition disclinations (from the mesh's curvature) only overlapped a few of the template's pentamers. One of the two is "more right" about the physical capsid, and which one tells you something real.

Nudge the one capsomer. Move vertex 90's position slightly toward where 89 sits, so the natural (Delaunay/intrinsic) triangulation makes 89 the degree-5 vertex on its own — no flips, no spawned defects.

Does that mean that the 3J3q structure has nothing to do with the derivation of  the C480 triangulation?No — it doesn't mean that. "Derived after the IDT/valence-flip steps" and "3J3Q had nothing to do with it" are different claims, and the gap between them is exactly where the interesting question lives.
Think of it as a chain, where each stage takes the previous one as input:
3J3Q alignment → seeds the 242 capsomer positions → Lloyd relaxation moves those positions → IDT builds connectivity from them → valence flips adjust that connectivity → C480_triangulation.txt.
So 3J3Q is upstream of the triangulation, not absent from it. Saying the pentamer assignment was "derived after the valence flips" tells you which stage stamped the final degree-5 labels onto specific vertices — but those vertices, and the positions that drove which ones became degree-5, trace back through Lloyd to the 3J3Q seeding. The valence flips didn't invent placement from nothing; they refined whatever the IDT produced, which in turn came from positions anchored on 3J3Q.
The right way to say it: 3J3Q influences the triangulation's pentamer placement indirectly and with attenuation. Each downstream stage can override the previous one's pentamer choices:

Lloyd relaxation moves capsomers to equalize cell areas — this can shift where curvature concentrates, so the pentamers the IDT later finds need not sit where 3J3Q put its 12.
The IDT picks degree-5 vertices from the relaxed geometry, not from 3J3Q labels.
The valence flips then push toward the {12×5, rest×6} target — and crucially, you told me earlier those flips shifted the pentamers off the original curvature peaks (your "0/12 match" observation). That's a stage actively relocating disclinations.

So by the time you reach C480_triangulation.txt, the 3J3Q fingerprint on pentamer placement has been filtered through three stages that each could move a pentamer. How much survives is genuinely unknown without checking — it could be "mostly 3J3Q with minor drift" or "Lloyd + flips substantially rewrote it."
This directly resolves what "template" meant in the 89/90 issue. The vertex-89 placement is the valence-flip stage's output, carrying an attenuated 3J3Q signal — not the raw 3J3Q placement, and not the raw mesh curvature either. That's why I listed three disagreeing references: the mesh curvature (charge partition), the triangulation's deg-5 vertices (89, i.e. the filtered-3J3Q answer), and flip-annihilation's preference (90). So all three scores are really measuring "smaller N = fewer cells to be irregular," which monotonically favors the floor. That's an artifact, not a determination.
Here's the honest conclusion: my current seeding-and-scoring cannot determine N for this spindle mesh. None of the three measures has an interior minimum; they all just slide to whichever boundary means "fewer points." I could hand you "H=200" but it would be the method's bias, not the mesh's preference, and that's exactly the kind of false answer that would burn you on the next mesh.
Let me think about why and what actually fixes it, because you want a general tool, not a number.
The root problem is that uniform area-equalizing Lloyd is the wrong seeder near disclinations and on high-aspect-ratio shapes. The thing that genuinely encodes N is not cell area — it's whether a consistent triangular lattice closes up around the 12 disclinations with no leftover frustration. That's a topological closure condition, and it's what the (k,l) Goldberg construction enforces exactly on a sphere. The reason your existing C480 worked is that the 3J3Q template supplied a lattice that already closes; my from-scratch Lloyd doesn't, at any N.
So the correct general method isn't "sweep N and score a geometry proxy." It's:

Seed the 12 disclinations from curvature (the charge partition — this part is solid and mesh-only).
For each candidate N, grow a geodesic triangular lattice outward from the disclinations (advancing-front / geodesic remeshing with target edge length = √(2A / (N·√3))), not uniform Lloyd. This produces a lattice that's locally hexagonal by construction, so leftover defects genuinely measure size-mismatch.
N = the size whose front closes with the fewest residual defects* — and that score will have an interior minimum, because too-small and too-large both leave a frustration ring where the fronts meet.

That advancing-front remesher is the missing piece — it's the "hardest, most research-active step" your own project notes flagged, and it's a real implementation effort (a few hundred lines, careful bookkeeping), not a quick swap. I don't want to fake it with another proxy.
Two honest options for how to proceed:

Anchor the sweep with a closure prior instead of a proxy. Goldberg–Coxeter says icosahedral shells exist only at N = 10(k²+kl+l²)+2, but a cone/spindle isn't icosahedral, so that exact rule doesn't bind — however, the area-derived N≈247 (from your measured 1.89M area at ~94 spacing) is a trustworthy prior, and it's independent of the broken proxies. I'd report H≈235, N≈247, C≈494 as the area-based estimate, clearly labeled as an estimate, and validate it by actually building and repairing that one size rather than trusting a sweep.
Build the advancing-front remesher so the sweep has a real closure signal. This is the right long-term answer for your "wide variety of meshes" goal, and I can start it now, but it's a substantial build and I'd want to test it on a synthetic geodesic (known N) before trusting it on the mesh.

Which do you want? I would go with option 2, build the advancing-front remesher, with the knowlege that the original mesh may not be fit precisely by the resulting fullerene.Architected advancing-front remesher with sphere testing and closure-based selectionArchitected advancing-front remesher with sphere testing and closure-based selectionGood — that's the right framing, and it's exactly the constraint that makes this honest: we're finding the fullerene whose lattice closes, not one that fits every bump of a resolution-limited mesh. That gap is the real physics you anticipated: a smooth, resolution-limited spindle has Gaussian curvature concentrated at its tips, so a perfectly commensurate hexagonal lattice with all 12 disclinations correctly placed may simply not exist for these exact coordinates — the surface and a closed fullerene lattice are slightly incompatible, exactly as you noted the mesh "may not be fit precisely." Second, the advancing front stalls intermittently (front-collision handling is greedy), which adds noise to the sweep; that's an engineering robustness issue, fixable but not yet fixed.
Where this leaves your general-tool goal. The architecture is right and reusable: curvature charge-partition for the 12 disclinations, area-derived h(N), advancing-front lattice, IDT + annealing repair, closure score for N-selection. The piece that needs hardening before it runs unattended on a large mesh series is the front-collision/stitching logic — making it deterministic and stall-free so the N-sweep is clean in one pass rather than needing multiple seeds. The three references, color-coded:

Orange — the 20 raw highest-curvature vertices: the literal sharpest spots on the mesh. Notice they cluster tightly at the two tips (and a couple of shoulder points), exactly as expected for a spindle — most of them pile onto just a few locations.

The measurement. The realized 5-folds sit a mean of 1.6 capsomer-spacings from the curvature centers (up to 2.5), with only 3 of 12 within one spacing. So your eye was right: the pentamers have largely not settled at the curvature peaks.
Why this happens — and it's the real scientific content here, not a bug. There are two competing "wants":
The orange peaks show that raw curvature is overwhelmingly concentrated at the tips. But a pentamer is a topological disclination carrying a fixed π/3 of charge regardless of local sharpness — so the lattice wants its 12 disclinations spread out to relieve strain evenly, not piled where the surface happens to be sharpest. The magenta centers are curvature's compromise (equal-charge partition); the red 5-folds are the lattice's compromise (advancing-front + relaxation minimizing connectivity strain). These two compromises disagree by ~1.6 spacings because the spindle is geometrically frustrated: there's no arrangement that simultaneously puts all 12 disclinations at curvature maxima and forms a clean hexagonal lattice. The lattice relaxation wins, pushing pentamers off the peaks toward positions that let the surrounding hexamer net close.
This is the same disagreement that surfaced earlier as the charge-partition-vs-template mismatch, now made visual: the smooth, resolution-limited mesh and a closed fullerene lattice genuinely prefer different pentamer placements.
Two caveats on the picture, in fairness: (1) this AFT structure still has residual defects (the anneal didn't fully clean it), so some red 5-folds are part of unresolved dislocation pairs and would move if the structure were driven to a perfect fullerene; and (2) "nearest 5-fold" is a loose correspondence since there are 16 red markers, not 12. So treat the 1.6-spacing figure as indicative of a large systematic offset, not a precise per-pentamer displacement.

Let me be precise about why, because it's the substantive finding:
The two residual heptagons sit 8 lattice steps / ~5.5 capsomer-spacings apart on opposite shoulders of the spindle. Edge flips can't destroy a (5,7) dipole in place — they can only glide it — so clearing these two requires walking a heptagon several rows across the lattice to meet a pentamer, and on this geometry every glide step spawns a replacement defect rather than reducing the count. There's no downhill path. This is exactly the geometric frustration we've been circling: the AFT lattice, grown purely from the spindle's curvature with no template to supply a commensurate starting connectivity, settles into a glassy state with a trapped scar — the same physics (Bowick–Nelson grain-boundary scars on curved crystals) that left residual on the sphere, but worse here because the spindle is more strongly and unevenly curved.
The contrast is the real point. Your template-derived C480 cleaned to exactly 12 because 3J3Q handed it a lattice that already nearly closes — it started one or two dipoles away from clean. The template-free AFT lattice does not close, because no perfectly commensurate hexagonal tiling with 12 well-placed disclinations exists for these coordinates. That's not a solver failure to apologize for; it's the answer to the question you posed two turns ago about whether the mesh and a clean fullerene want the same thing. They don't.

Let me be precise about what I did and why it's wrong for your criterion.
The original 12 points came from the charge partition, which uses Gaussian curvature (angle defect) and then takes the centroid of each equal-charge region. Two problems with that for picking bowl points: (1) Gaussian curvature K = κ₁·κ₂ is also positive at points where the surface is convex in only an averaged sense, and the region centroid can land in a flat or saddle spot even if the region's charge is real; (2) on a spindle the body regions are large and low-charge, so their centroids drift into cylinder-like (ridge) territory where one principal curvature is near zero — not bowl-shaped at all. I never required both principal curvatures to be positive. Let me fix that — compute true principal curvatures and re-pick using min(κ₁, κ₂) as the bowl criterion.Compute per-vertex principal curvatures and check how bowl-shaped the current 12 points areCompute per-vertex principal curvatures and check how bowl-shaped the current 12 points areYour eye was exactly right. The principal-curvature analysis confirms it: centroids 8 and 10 are saddle points (κ_min < 0), and 7 and 11 are borderline ridges (κ_min ≈ 0.001 and ≈ 0) — these are the points sitting away from any bowl. The other 8 are genuine bowls. So the charge-partition centroid method placed ~4 of 12 in non-bowl locations, just as you observed visually. Your diagnosis was correct. The original charge-partition method placed ~4 of 12 points poorly: vertices 8 and 10 were literal saddle points (κ_min < 0), and 7 and 11 were near-flat ridges (κ_min ≈ 0). Two honest notes. First, the lattice settled at N=216 capsomers (C428), smaller than the N≈232 the earlier noisy sweep suggested — the bowl seeding preferred a more commensurate size, which I'd now trust over the earlier estimate. Second, the geometry still told us something real along the way: only 6 of the 12 bowls are strong (κ_min ≈ 0.005–0.007 near the tips/shoulders); the other 6 are weak bowls (κ_min ≈ 0.0004–0.0015) on the cylinder-like body. A spindle genuinely doesn't have 12 strongly bowl-shaped spots — but requiring even weak positive κ_min on both axes was enough to avoid the saddle traps and let the lattice close.

Let me verify the exact radii rather than eyeball it.Compare capsomer vs dual-cage radii to diagnose why spiral floatsCompare capsomer vs dual-cage radii to diagnose why spiral floatsThat confirms both problems. Here's what's going on:
1. Why it floats above the cage — a scaling bug I introduced. In Blender I pushed the spiral outward by a factor of 1.10 from the centroid. On a sphere that's a uniform 10% lift, but this is a spindle where the tips reach radius ~654 vs a mean of ~400. A multiplicative 1.10 push adds 0.10 × 654 ≈ 65 units of lift at the tips but only ~24 at the waist — so the spiral balloons away from the cage most at exactly the two ends, which is what you see. It should have been a small additive offset (constant clearance), or no offset at all.
2. Why it misses the polygon centers — a definitional mismatch. The spiral is drawn through the capsomer positions (P, the triangulation vertices), but the cage you're seeing is the dual — its pentagon/hexagon faces are centered on those same capsomers, while its drawn vertices (the gray polygon corners) are the triangle centers. So the spiral path nodes correspond to face centers, but I then smoothed the dual vertices onto a slightly different shell (radius mean 449 vs the capsomers' 400) and lifted the spiral on top of that — so the path no longer lands in the visible polygon centers. The spiral and the cage ended up on two different radial shells.
The fix is to draw the spiral with no multiplicative push, sitting exactly on the capsomer positions (which are the polygon centers), so it threads through the middle of each pentagon and hexagon and hugs the cage at the tips. Let me redo it.Used 2 toolsUsed 2 toolsNow the spiral sits on the cage — it threads through the polygon centers and hugs the surface, with no float at the tips. But it now has the opposite problem: the tube is at the same radius as the cage faces, so the gray hexagons occlude most of it (you only see the cyan segments that face the camera, and the path looks broken). That's expected with the tube buried in the shell.
So the honest tradeoff is: on the cage = occluded; lifted = floats (especially at the tips with a multiplicative push). The right answer is a small constant additive clearance — a few units straight outward, the same amount everywhere — so the tube rides just proud of the faces and reads as a continuous path without ballooning at the ends. Let me do that.Used 2 toolsUsed 2 toolsThat's the fix. The spiral now rides a uniform ~22 units above the cage everywhere — continuous from the narrow tip (green start) all the way around to the wide end, threading over the polygon centers and hugging the surface at both ends instead of ballooning away.
To answer your question directly, it was two bugs in how I drew it, not anything about the structure:
The floating at the ends came from a multiplicative 1.10× push from the centroid. Because the spindle tips reach radius ~654 vs ~400 at the waist, a 10% scale adds ~65 units of lift at the tips but only ~24 at the waist — so it flared open precisely where the surface is most extended. Replacing that with a constant additive offset (the same 22 units in the outward direction at every node) gives uniform clearance.
The missing the polygon centers was a shell mismatch: the spiral nodes are the capsomer positions (which are the pentagon/hexagon centers, mean radius ~400), but I'd smoothed the visible dual cage onto a slightly larger shell (mean ~449) and then lifted the spiral on top of that — so the two ended up on different radii. Drawing the spiral from the capsomer positions with a small fixed clearance puts it back where it belongs, right over the face centers.

Let me address them in order, starting by checking whether the "3 adjacent pentamers" is real, because if it is, that's a genuine validity problem, not a cosmetic one.Check for adjacent pentamers (IPR violations) and locate clustersCheck for adjacent pentamers (IPR violations) and locate clustersYour eye was right — there's a real 3-pentamer cluster (204–205–206, all mutually chained), an IPR violation. One correction: it's at the narrow end (t ≈ −566 to −613), not the broad end as it appeared on screen. So you've found two genuine defects, and both trace to the same root cause you're asking about. Let me answer all three points.
1. The 3-adjacent pentamers (IPR violation). This is real and it's a quality flaw, though still a topologically valid fullerene (12 pentagons, just clustered). It happens because the AFT lattice is too coarse/strained at the narrow tip — when a cone tapers sharply, the seeder crams disclinations together and the repair has no room to separate them. Large viral capsids are almost always IPR (isolated pentagons), so this clustering is a sign the lattice isn't capturing the true cap geometry.
2. Unequal edge lengths. Let me quantify rather than assert.Quantify edge-length variation and compare to mesh and radius variationQuantify edge-length variation and compare to mesh and radius variationThe edge lengths vary a lot — CV 23.7%, max/min ≈ 3.9×. You're right that a true fullerene has near-equal edges. But here's the important distinction for your remeshing question:
There are two separate sources of unequal edges, and remeshing only fixes one of them:

Source A — the spindle shape itself. A real fullerene embeds as a near-sphere where all edges are ~equal. But you're forcing the lattice onto a spindle whose radius varies 29% (tips far out, waist near in). Any near-equal-area capsomer tiling on that shape must have unequal 3D edge lengths — the geometry demands it. Remeshing cannot fix this; it's intrinsic to fitting a fullerene to a non-spherical surface. (The chemist's "equal edges" assumes the molecule relaxes to its own preferred shape; your capsid is constrained to the cryo-ET envelope.)
Source B — the capsomer placement quality. On top of the unavoidable shape variation, my AFT seeding adds noise: capsomers placed too close or too far, which is what produces the 63–249 spread and the pentamer clustering. This is what remeshing/better-seeding can fix.

3. Your actual question — does the mesh4a vertex distribution influence the result, and would remeshing help? Yes, substantially, and here's the mechanism. My pipeline snaps capsomer radii and the advancing front to the nearest mesh4a vertices. The original mesh has triangle edges varying 59% (max/min ≈ 25×) — it's a very uneven segmentation, dense in some patches and sparse in others. So the AFT inherits that unevenness: where the mesh is sparse (likely the smooth tips), capsomers get placed irregularly, which is exactly where the pentamer cluster formed.
So remeshing mesh4a to uniform triangles would help — it would remove Source B's mesh-induced noise, very likely separate the clustered pentamers, and tighten the edge-length spread toward the irreducible Source-A floor (~the 29% set by the shape). It would not give perfectly equal edges, because the spindle shape forbids that.
Let me test this directly: remesh mesh4a to uniform edge length (isotropic remeshing via repeated edge-split/collapse/flip toward a target length, projected back to the surface), then re-run the pipeline and compare.Isotropic remesh mesh4a toward uniform edge length and check uniformity improvementIsotropic remesh mesh4a toward uniform edge length and check uniformity improvementMy remesher is broken — Euler = −30 (should be 2), and it created holes (149 faces from 193 verts is far too few). The 1→3 split with a single-edge fallback tears the mesh; collapses create non-manifold spots. Writing a robust isotropic remesher from scratch is genuinely involved (the half-edge bookkeeping is exactly what libraries like PyMeshLab/pymeshlab's meshing_isotropic_explicit_remeshing exist to handle), and I don't have those here.
Rather than ship a broken remesher, let me use a sounder approach that achieves the same goal — uniform sampling — without fragile topology surgery: generate a uniform point set directly on the radius field (the spindle surface) by farthest-point sampling at fixed geodesic spacing, independent of mesh4a's uneven vertices. This sidesteps the segmentation's unevenness entirely, which is the real fix for your question.Build uniform capsomer sampling independent of mesh4a vertices; check IPR and edge uniformityBuild uniform capsomer sampling independent of mesh4a vertices; check IPR and edge uniformityTimed out — the farthest-point sampling over 60k candidates with per-query radius lookups is too slow. Let me cut the candidate pool and vectorize the radius lookup.Faster uniform sampling test with vectorized radius lookupFaster uniform sampling test with vectorized radius lookupInteresting and honest result: uniform sampling did not help — it gave residual 6 (not clean), 3 IPR violations (worse), and higher edge CV (34.7% vs 23.7%). This is a real finding, not a failure to brush aside. Let me interpret it carefully, because it answers your question precisely.
Why remeshing/uniform sampling didn't help — and what actually drives the problems:
The mesh4a vertices turn out to matter less than I expected. When I sampled uniformly (ignoring mesh4a's vertices entirely), the result got slightly worse. The reason: my "uniform" farthest-point sampling equalizes spacing on the spindle surface, but the spindle's curvature is wildly non-uniform, so equal-spacing-in-3D actively fights the lattice. The earlier bowl-seeded version was better precisely because it concentrated points appropriately at the caps. So uniform isn't the goal — curvature-appropriate density is.
This reframes your three problems:

The 3-pentamer cluster isn't caused by mesh4a's uneven triangulation. It's caused by the sharp taper at the narrow tip: a cone apex sharper than ~38° genuinely requires 5+ pentagons crowded together, and if it's sharp enough they're forced adjacent. The clustering may be telling you something true about that tip's geometry — or that the tip is a segmentation artifact (over-sharpened by low resolution). Either way, remeshing won't fix it; it's set by the tip's cone angle.
Unequal edges are dominated by Source A (the spindle shape), not Source B (mesh sampling). The radius varies 29%, and no remeshing changes the shape. Uniform 3D sampling made edges worse because it ignored that the caps need denser, shorter edges than the body. Equal edges are simply impossible on a 29%-radius-varying surface.
How much does mesh4a influence it? Less than the shape does. Both the mesh-based and the mesh-independent runs land in the same quality regime (CV 24–35%, a few defects), because the dominant constraint is the spindle geometry, which both share. The segmentation noise is a secondary effect.

So the honest answer to your question: remeshing mesh4a to uniform triangles would not meaningfully fix the gaps or the edge inequality, because both are driven by the spindle's intrinsic shape, not by the mesh's vertex distribution. The lever that would help is the opposite of uniform — a curvature-adaptive capsomer density (more, smaller cells at the high-curvature caps; fewer, larger on the body), which is what naturally produces isolated pentagons and the most uniform-possible edges on a given shape.
Two things I'd genuinely suggest, if you want to push further:

Test whether the narrow tip is real. If that cap's cone angle in mesh4a is sharper than physically plausible for HIV, the 3-pentamer cluster is a segmentation artifact, and smoothing/regularizing just that tip (not the whole mesh) would let the pentagons separate. This is checkable: I can measure the tip's cone half-angle and compare to what 5 vs 6 vs 7 pentagons at a cap implies.
Switch the seeding objective from "equal area/spacing" to "constant intrinsic (angular) spacing" — i.e. equal capsomers per unit solid angle from the centroid rather than per unit surface area. On a spindle these differ a lot, and the angular version tends to place caps correctly without crowding.

Which would you like — measure the tip angle to test the artifact hypothesis, or try the angular-spacing seeding? I'd lean toward the tip measurement first, since it tells you whether you're fighting real geometry or a data artifact before investing in more remeshing.you say that the spindle shape cannot represent a true fullerene. What's actually true is narrow: for a fixed set of 3D vertex positions lying on a 29%-varying-radius surface, the straight-line (chord) edge lengths between those fixed points can't all be equal. That's just a statement about chords between pinned points.
But that is not what a true fullerene requires, and I conflated two different things. A real HIV capsid fullerene has equal edge connectivity and locally equal intrinsic (geodesic/relaxed) edge lengths — the protein subunits are essentially uniform, and the lattice is locally hexagonal everywhere. The cone/spindle shape arises precisely from a valid fullerene tiling: you distribute the 12 pentagons appropriately (classically 5 at one cap, 7 at the other for the canonical cone) and the hexagonal lattice between them naturally closes into a tube-with-caps. The Caspar–Klug / cone-capsid work (Ganser, Nguyen, Sundquist; later Bharat, Briggs cryo-ET) shows exactly this — genuine, equal-subunit fullerene cones and spindles. So spindle shape and true fullerene geometry are not in tension at all; the shape is a consequence of where the pentamers sit.
Where I went wrong: I measured the chord lengths between my capsomer points and saw 24–35% variation, then wrongly elevated that into a claim about fullerene geometry itself. The real situation is the reverse of what I said. A proper fullerene cone does have uniform subunits — and the fact that my reconstruction shows unequal edges and clustered pentagons is evidence that my reconstruction is wrong, not that the shape can't host a true fullerene. The variation is an artifact of my pipeline (poor capsomer placement, a too-coarse/strained tip), not an intrinsic obstacle.
This also flips the conclusion from my last message. I argued remeshing wouldn't help because "the shape forbids equal edges." That reasoning was based on the bad premise. Better/adaptive remeshing and correct pentamer placement should move toward the genuine equal-edge tiling, not away from it.

Does that match how you'd judge whether a reconstruction is correct?This is exactly correct.  The interviening hexagons should be regular, but can follow a conformal curve.That lands it, and "conformal" is the precise word that ties the whole thread together. Let me reflect back the objective in geometric terms to make sure my understanding is exact, because it changes what the pipeline should optimize.
A conformal map preserves angles and local shape, not absolute size. So the right criterion isn't equal chord lengths (which I wrongly chased) — it's that each hexamer remains a regular hexagon in its local intrinsic frame: equal angles, locally equidistant neighbors, the lattice locally indistinguishable from a flat honeycomb. The cells are then free to scale up or down as they ride over the curved surface — larger where the body is fat, smaller and angularly compressed at the tapered caps — exactly as a conformal grid does. The 12 pentamers are the conical singularities where that conformal hexagonal sheet is forced to cone (each absorbing π/3 of angle deficit), and they sit wherever curvature concentrates, isolated (IPR), with no prescribed end-count.
This means my entire success metric was wrong, and I can now state the correct one:

Wrong (what I measured): 3D chord-edge-length uniformity. This penalizes the very size variation a conformal tiling is supposed to have, which is why it "looked bad" on a legitimately valid structure.
Right (what I should measure): local regularity in the intrinsic metric — per-vertex, the incident angles around each hexamer ≈ 360°/(its neighbor count) and the neighbor distances equal to each other locally (not globally), i.e. each cell is locally isotropic. Plus IPR (12 isolated pentamers) and a clean locally-hexagonal connectivity. Absolute cell size follows the conformal scale factor and is not penalized.

Concretely, this reshapes the algorithm in two ways. First, seeding: keep the curvature-driven charge-partition for the 12 pentamers (it respects "follow the curvature, don't prescribe an end-split") and drop the hard cap-clustering heuristic that caused the IPR violation. Second — and this is the piece that's genuinely missing — a conformal relaxation of the capsomer positions: instead of pinning vertices to the mesh and measuring chords, let them slide along the surface to equalize each cell's angular regularity (a tangential, area-preserving-up-to-scale relaxation, like a conformal Lloyd / centroidal step in the intrinsic metric), while staying on the cryo-ET envelope. That naturally produces regular hexagons that grow and shrink conformally and lets the pentamers settle into isolated curvature wells. The success check becomes the angle-regularity residual and IPR, not chord CV.
Before I build that, two points I want to get right from you, since the definitions matter:

Second, for the regularity residual — is angle-equality (each hexamer's six internal angles equal in the surface metric) the property you'd actually inspect to call a reconstruction correct, or would you also want near-equal local edge lengths within each cell (local isotropy), allowing the scale to drift only between cells rather than within one?There are two parameters that define the relationships between hexamers, the bend angle and the twist angle.  In HIV Capsids the bend (or dihedral angle) is more flexible, while any twist between to hexamers is rather much more limited, but not necessarily 0 degrees.That's the key physical constraint, and it sharpens the objective considerably. Let me make sure I have the two degrees of freedom right, because they map onto distinct terms in the relaxation.
For an edge shared by two hexamers, taking the local frame at that edge:

Bend (dihedral) — the fold between the two hexamer planes about their shared edge, i.e. the out-of-plane hinge. This is the compliant mode: it's how the sheet accumulates the Gaussian curvature to wrap a cone/spindle, and it varies freely from the fat body (near-flat, small bend) to the tapered caps (large bend). It should be essentially unpenalized — it's the conformal scale/curvature mode expressing itself.
Twist — the relative rotation of the two hexamers about the shared edge's in-surface normal direction (the screw/skew between adjacent capsomer frames, the in-plane shear of the lattice). This is the stiff mode: tightly constrained, near-zero but not exactly zero — a small allowed nonzero twist, presumably with a narrow tolerance.

So the relaxation energy isn't "equal edges" or even pure angle-regularity — it's anisotropic in exactly these two modes: soft spring on bend, stiff spring on twist (penalizing |twist| beyond its small allowed band), with the lattice connectivity (IPR, locally hexagonal) fixed. That's a much more physical and better-posed target than chord uniformity — it lets the surface bend into whatever spindle/cone the curvature demands while forbidding the shear that real capsomer–capsomer interfaces resist. The pentamers remain the 12 curvature singularities, placed by charge partition, isolated, end-split unprescribed.
This reframes the success metric cleanly: a correct reconstruction is one where, after relaxation, the twist residual across every hexamer–hexamer edge stays within the allowed band, while bend is free to vary smoothly. Twist concentrated anywhere (or exceeding tolerance) flags a misplaced capsomer or a wrong local topology — far more diagnostic than the edge-length CV I was wrongly using.

Twist between neighbors is the misalignment of their in-plane axes after accounting for the bend (parallel-transporting one frame across the shared edge to the other). What the literature confirms for the relaxation design: the capsid is a variably-curved fullerene where, per the Pornillos/Ganser/Yeager hexamer work, the dihedral angles between adjacent hexamer planes vary gradually and continuously along the body of the cone, but change more drastically at the ends, immediately adjacent to the pentamers. That's exactly the bend/twist split you described — bend is the compliant mode that ramps up near the pentamers, and the curvature is accommodated by plasticity at the intersubunit interfaces. The Mattei cryo-ET work adds the operational point that matters for your pipeline: the pentamer forms using different interfaces than the hexamer, and they could place every individual hexamer and pentamer — so the pentamer interface geometry is genuinely distinct from the hexamer one, which means the cap (pentamer-adjacent) angles should be modeled separately from body hexamer–hexamer angles, just as you said. Cell PressScience
The one I want to flag before building: a very recent (Dec 2025) bioRxiv preprint argues that the idealized fullerene may be the wrong backbone to fit. It reports that local rotations and positional adjustments of hexamers and pentamers result in imperfect alignment between adjacent capsomers, forming a pseudo-tiling rather than a perfectly aligned fullerene network, and that hexagons in fullerene cones are typically distorted because they are part of a surface with triangular gaps between capsomers. In other words: real capsomers don't tile edge-to-edge; there are triangular gaps at the threefold axes, and forcing a gap-free fullerene introduces exactly the kind of twist/misalignment you want to minimize. This is worth your awareness because your "minimize twist as hard as possible" objective and their "pseudo-tiling with gaps" observation are two views of the same phenomenon — the residual twist I'd be minimizing may be physically real interface rotation, not just reconstruction error. bioRxivbiorxiv

Two things from the full text that bear directly on what you're building:
First, the paper's own twist framing is more specific than the abstract suggested. Their "pseudo-tiling" is explicitly an intermediate between the idealized fullerene and a Kagome lattice: the hexagons and pentagons in the Kagome lattice are rotated by ∼30° relative to the fullerene lattice, while those in the data-derived lattice exhibit an intermediate rotation – less than 30°. So the "twist" they observe is a bounded in-plane rotation between 0° and 30°, not unbounded — which is consistent with your statement that twist is limited but not necessarily zero. That gives a concrete upper bound (well under 30°) if you want one for the stiff-spring tolerance. bioRxiv
Second, and worth flagging for your pipeline specifically: this paper used the Fullerene program (Fowler–Manolopoulos lineage) to generate cone backbones, and notes that the Fullerene program generated models by adjusting ring spiral pentagon indices (RSPIs) in small increments, with validity tested on carbon count and RSPI rules. That's the same RSPI machinery we were just using — so their C352/C452/C524 cone datasets are directly comparable to your C428, and their generation-by-RSPI-increment approach is essentially a more mature version of what your tool is doing. bioRxiv
One honest caveat: the paper's framing is that the idealized fullerene is not the most faithful representation — only the data-derived surface lattice links potential seam-line locations with local frustration patterns, in contrast to the conventionally used fullerene lattice. So if your end goal is biophysical fidelity rather than a clean fullerene backbone, this paper is arguing the backbone itself should carry the bounded twist, not have it minimized away. That's a real tension with the "minimize twist as hard as possible" objective you described — it may be worth deciding whether you want the idealized fullerene backbone (twist→minimal) as a reference to measure deviation against, or the data-derived lattice (twist retained) as the target. The regularity constraint is too soft, so "minimize twist" degenerates into "distort cells." A correct version needs regularity (and probably near-equal local edge lengths) as a much stronger constraint, or a hard one, so twist is minimized subject to the lattice staying regular — which is what you actually specified.
Pure per-vertex gradient descent on this coupled, frame-dependent energy is fragile and slow. A better formulation would treat it as a global least-squares (align all neighboring frames simultaneously) rather than greedy local steps.

Here's the thing I need to admit: I've been solving the wrong problem the whole time. My entire pipeline does this:

Then conformally fit that perfect cage onto the mesh4a envelope, with bend free and twist minimized — the relaxation I built becomes the final step on an already-correct topology, not a doomed attempt to rescue a bad one.

My approach (mesh → triangulate → dual) was always going to produce irregular, defect-prone tilings because it derives connectivity from noisy point placement. The generate-then-fit approach produces a guaranteed-valid fullerene and uses the mesh only to pick which isomer and how to drape it.

I had been treating "curvature concentrates at the caps" as if the disclinations must live at the two ends. But that conflates two different things, and the Twarock second example (the more spindle-like core) shows why they're separable. A regular hexagonal sheet is flat (zero Gaussian curvature) everywhere except at its 12 pentagonal disclinations — each pentagon injects exactly π/3 of curvature. Where you place those 12 disclinations determines the 3D shape. If you cluster them at two ends, you get a classic cone. But if you distribute some of them along the body — including 3 or so in the middle — you get a spindle whose curvature is genuinely spread out, with bowls in the midsection, not just at the tips.
So the mid-body bowls aren't a problem for regular tiling at all — they're pentamers sitting in the middle, and the surface curves there precisely because a disclination is there. The curvature follows the pentamers, not the other way around. A fully regular, equal-cell tiling produces exactly this spindle when 3 of the 12 pentagons are placed mid-body. That's the insight I was missing: I kept thinking mid-body curvature meant "irregular cells forced by the shape," when in fact it means "a pentamer belongs there, and the regular sheet bends around it."
This also explains why my κ_min bowl-finder kept flagging mid-body bowls and why I wrongly tried to push them to the caps — those mid-body bowls are the correct pentamer sites. The earlier instinct to cluster 6/6 at the ends was imposing a cone when the data wants a spindle with interior disclinations.
So the corrected picture, which I now think is fully consistent:

12 pentagons, placed wherever the κ_min bowls are — some at caps, at least ~3 mid-body — no prescription to cluster at ends.
All cells regular and equal-sized — entirely compatible with a spindle, because the spindle shape emerges from the disclination placement, not from cell-size variation.
The bowls (curvature maxima) are literally the projections of where the regular sheet has to cone — so curvature placement and regular tiling are the same constraint, not competing ones.

Let me make sure I state it as the actual algorithm, because your constraint determines the whole structure, and then flag the one place where it genuinely bites.
The procedure, depending only on bowl curvature:

Find all κ_min bowls on the mesh (the local maxima of minimum principal curvature — points convex in both directions). This is pure geometry, no shape model.
The 12 strongest bowls are the pentamer sites — wherever they fall, caps or mid-body. By Gauss–Bonnet there are always exactly 12 net disclinations on a closed genus-0 surface, so the curvature must integrate to 12 π/3 quanta; the bowls are where they localize. No prescription about caps vs. middle — the mesh decides.
Grow a regular hexagonal sheet with pentagonal disclinations at those 12 sites, equal cells by construction.
Drape toward the envelope, bend free, twist minimized.

This is shape-agnostic: a cone, a spindle, a capsule, and a near-sphere all emerge from the same procedure, differing only in where their bowls happen to be. That's the generality you want, and it's the right anchor.
Now the one place I have to be honest, because it's a real obstacle and not something I should paper over: steps 2 and 3 are in tension, and resolving that tension is the crux of the whole inverse problem.
The bowls give 12 positions in space. A regular tiling gives 12 disclinations at specific spiral (RSPI) positions. For an arbitrary set of 12 spatial points, there is generally no perfectly regular equal-cell fullerene whose pentagons land exactly on them — because the regular sheet's disclinations can only sit at lattice-commensurate locations, and 12 arbitrary bowl points won't be mutually commensurate. So the procedure must find the regular tiling whose 12 pentagons land as close as possible to the 12 bowls — a best fit, not an exact match. That's well-defined and shape-agnostic, but it means "regular cells" and "pentagons exactly on bowls" can't both be exactly satisfied, and the procedure has to trade them off (you've already told me regularity wins, mesh-fit is approximate — so: pentagons near bowls, cells exactly regular).
That trade-off is precisely the local RSPI search we converged on, now stated generally and shape-free:

Given the 12 bowl directions from the mesh, search the local neighborhood of fullerene isomers (capsomer total free in 200–300) for the one whose 12 pentagons, when the regular cage is draped on the envelope, sit closest to the bowl directions — subject to IPR and twist ≤ 30°. Select by bowl-proximity + drape-closeness.

No shape assumptions, no enumeration of billions (the bowls localize the search), no tailoring to spindle vs. cone — just "12 bowls in, best-fitting regular fullerene out."

The hexagon/pentagon edge length is a free scale parameter, fixed only indirectly: for a given mesh envelope (fixed surface area, roughly), choosing the edge length is equivalent to choosing how many tiles cover it — larger edges → fewer, larger cells → fewer total capsomers; smaller edges → more. So edge length and capsomer count are the same knob viewed two ways, and the [200, 300] constraint is really a constraint on that single scale parameter. The tiles stay equal-sized (regularity preserved); what varies between candidate solutions is the common edge length they all share.
This means the search has two coupled free parameters, both shape-agnostic:

Scale (edge length ↔ total capsomer count in [200, 300]) — one global number.
Isomer (which RSPI, i.e. where the 12 disclinations sit) — selected by proximity to the 12 bowls.

And the selection criterion ties them together: across candidate (scale, isomer) pairs, pick the one whose regular cage, draped on the envelope, puts its 12 pentagons closest to the 12 κ_min bowls while staying IPR and twist ≤ 30°. The edge length isn't pinned a priori — it's chosen as the value (within the [200,300] band) that lets a regular cage best straddle the envelope with pentagons on the bowls. So I'll sweep scale rather than fix it, exactly as you'd want for a general procedure.
One consequence worth noting, still shape-free: because edge length is free, the procedure shouldn't try to match the mesh's absolute size or local spacing — only its shape (the bowl directions and the envelope form). Two capsids of different physical size but the same shape should yield the same isomer at proportionally scaled edge lengths. That's a good invariance to have and confirms the procedure depends only on the curvature geometry, not on scale-specific mesh details.
So the finalized procedure, depending only on bowl curvature + the [200,300] band:

Extract all κ_min bowls; take the 12 strongest as disclination targets (directions, scale-free).

Select the (scale, isomer) minimizing bowl-misalignment + envelope deviation, subject to IPR and twist ≤ 30°.

That's the structure underneath the "billions of RSPI" worry you raised: the space of valid structures isn't an unstructured combinatorial explosion, it's a lattice, and the cone metric (the actual shape) picks out a neighborhood in it.

Let me be straight about the core problem rather than keep tuning weights. The issue is that I keep generating the point set by Lloyd (equal-area) sampling and then triangulating, which scatters disclinations everywhere. The actual Thurston construction does the opposite: it never scatters defects because it builds the lattice from a flat developing map — the hexagonal sheet is laid out in the Euclidean plane (where it's perfectly regular, zero defects), and the 12 cone points are introduced by cutting wedges and gluing (§7 of the paper: the isosceles-triangle / butterfly construction), so curvature appears only at the 12 cones, exactly π/3 each, by construction. There is no glassiness because the lattice is never relaxed into a glassy state — it's regular from the start.
So my Lloyd-then-Delaunay approach is fundamentally not the Thurston method; it's the old approach with cone-pinning bolted on. To actually implement Thurston I need the developing-map construction, which is more involved but is the thing that guarantees regularity. But your question makes me think you may be pointing at something I'd otherwise miss — so let me ask rather than assume, because there's a real way the hyperbolic layer would matter:

Thurston's answer is that those aren't a formless combinatorial sea — they're lattice points in ℂ^(1,9), and the cone metric (the mesh's actual shape) selects a neighborhood in that lattice. If I want to do a principled local search — "find the triangulation nearest the one my envelope induces, and enumerate its neighbors without redundancy" — the natural coordinate system for that search is the hyperbolic one (the butterfly moves are reflections generating Γ, the fundamental domain weeds out repetitions). Is that what you're pointing at?Yes indeed.  For ultimate computational practicality it would seem that the hyperbolic method would be preferred.Agreed, and now I can state the architecture precisely, with the honest caveats about what's hard.
The hyperbolic method gives the general-case procedure its backbone. The reason it's the practical choice is exactly Thurston's Theorem 0.1: the triangulations are lattice points in ℂ^(1,9), so instead of enumerating isomers (the "billions" problem), you compute one point — the lattice point your mesh induces — and read off the nearest valid triangulation. The mesh's cone metric maps to a point in CH⁹; the nearest lattice point in L⁺/Γ is the answer; the carbon count is its squared norm. That's an O(1) lookup plus a bounded local search in a fundamental domain, not a combinatorial sweep. For many meshes, that's the difference between feasible and not.
So the full procedure, depending only on bowl curvature:

Mesh → cone metric. 12 bowls give 12 cone points of curvature π/3 (Gauss–Bonnet guarantees the sum is 4π). This is geometry I already extract.
Cone metric → ℂ^(1,9) coordinates. Develop the metric, compute the cocycle Z (Thurston §3): each triangulation/metric is a vector whose area-form value (signature (1,9)) is the triangle count. This is the embedding into the complex Lorentz space.
Project to the lattice. Find the nearest lattice point in L⁺/Γ — reduce into the fundamental domain via butterfly moves (the generators of Γ). That lattice point is a valid fullerene triangulation, regular by construction, with curvature only at the 12 cones.
Read off the result. Carbon count = squared norm; extract RSPI for identification; develop back to 3D coordinates and drape on the envelope.

Now the honest part, because this is a serious build and I don't want to oversell what I can deliver in this sandbox:
The hard step is 2→3 — computing the cocycle coordinates and implementing the Γ-reduction (butterfly moves) correctly. Thurston gives the theory cleanly, but the explicit lattice reduction in ℂ^(1,9) with the signature-(1,9) form is intricate, and a subtle bug produces a plausible-looking but invalid triangulation. This is real research-grade implementation, not a quick script. I'd want to build it incrementally and validate each stage against a known mathematical case (e.g. develop the icosahedron — P(n;1,…,1) — and confirm it lands on the expected lattice point), using no data from your Fullerene files.
There's also a genuine mathematical subtlety I should flag: the lattice point gives a triangulation that is intrinsically regular (perfect in the cone metric), but its 3D embedding on your specific envelope is a separate draping step, and the two won't coincide exactly — which you've already accepted.

The next stages, in order:
Stage 2 — the holonomy and the ℂ^(1,n−3) coordinates. Problem 2 — sheared hexagons. This is the more important one and it's a real flaw in my relaxer. I used bond-length springs only, which equalize edges but place no constraint on angles. So hexagons satisfy "all 6 sides equal" while shearing into rhombus/rectangular shapes — interior-angle std averages 19.8°, up to 64°. Equal edges ≠ regular hexagon. A regular hexagon needs equal edges and all interior angles 120°.

I need to be honest about what this reveals. My spring-based relaxers keep failing the same way: they're ad-hoc force balances with no guarantee of producing a valid convex polytope, so they fall into whatever local minimum (buckled, or over-smoothed) the weights happen to favor. I've now hit this from several angles. The right tool for "given fullerene connectivity, produce the correct convex 3D embedding" is not a spring relaxer at all — it's a principled method:
The standard, reliable approach is the Tutte/Kamada–Kawai spectral embedding followed by spherical projection — or exactly what your Fullerene program's Iopt force field does. Fullerene-cage embedding is a known-hard numerical problem that the Fowler–Manolopoulos code solves properly (their force field includes the right combination of bond, angle, and dihedral/convexity terms tuned over decades). My from-scratch springs are not going to match it, as these repeated failures show.

My side (mesh-only): extract bowls → connectivity → RSPI. That's the inverse-problem part, and it's done: RSPI = [1, 25, 43, 44, 54, 70, 174, 176, 188, 197, 200, 213], C428.
Your side (validated): feed that RSPI to the Fullerene program → it produces the correct convex, regular 3D cage.

Second, and more fundamentally: an arbitrary RSPI does not produce a valid fullerene. Most 12-tuples of spiral indices fail — the spiral either doesn't close, produces wrong face sizes, or isn't realizable as a cubic planar graph at all. The fraction of valid RSPIs shrinks rapidly with size. So "extract an RSPI from the mesh and feed it to the Fullerene program" is circular in a way I glossed over: producing a valid RSPI is itself the hard problem, and a noisy mesh won't hand me one for free.
This is exactly the objection you raised many turns ago about the billions of RSPI, and I partially lost sight of it. Let me state the situation honestly, because it clarifies what the real method has to be:
The RSPI I extracted for C428 is valid — but only because it came from an already-valid triangulation (the AFT-built dual). The AFT did the hard work of producing a closed, defect-free triangulation; the RSPI was just read off the end. So the actual general procedure is not "mesh → RSPI → Fullerene program." It's "mesh → valid triangulation → (RSPI is then a derived label)." The valid-triangulation step is the whole game, and the RSPI is a byproduct, not the engine.
Which means the genuine generality lives in the step that produces a valid closed triangulation with exactly 12 disclinations from an arbitrary mesh — and that is precisely the step that has resisted every clean method I tried:

The AFT advancing-front did produce valid triangulations (it gave clean C428), and it ran on the mesh generically — but it's a heuristic, and on a different mesh it might leave defects or clusters.
The Thurston cone-metric method is the principled general answer (any 12 cone points of π/3 → a valid triangulation by construction), but I could not complete the ℂ^(1,9) lattice reduction that makes it work for arbitrary (non-commensurate) bowl configurations.
Relaxation methods go glassy.

So your instinct is correct: nothing I've delivered is yet a general solution. What I have is (a) a heuristic (AFT) that empirically produces valid triangulations but without guarantee, and (b) a principled method (Thurston) that's incomplete at the hardest step.
Let me be straight about what a genuinely general procedure requires, so we're not fooling ourselves:
It needs a step that, for any cryo-ET mesh, reliably produces a closed triangulation where every vertex is degree 5 or 6, with exactly 12 degree-5 vertices placed near the curvature bowls — and does so robustly, not just on mesh4a. The two candidates are: make the AFT robust enough to trust across many meshes (engineering, testable), or complete the Thurston lattice reduction (principled, but the step that defeated me four times). The RSPI/Fullerene-program handoff is only the final labeling/embedding convenience once that valid triangulation exists — it is not, and I should not have implied it was, the general engine.
So the honest reframing of where the generality must come from:
The robust general step is producing the valid triangulation directly on the mesh (AFT-style or Thurston), and then the triangulation is the answer — its dual is the fullerene, and its 3D coordinates come from the capsomer positions already on the mesh (no separate embedding needed, no RSPI round-trip required). The RSPI is only useful as a compact identifier or if you specifically want your Fullerene program to re-embed it.
Given that, the productive question is which general triangulation-builder to invest in:
The AFT already ran generically on mesh4a from the bowls and produced a valid result; its weakness is no guarantee (the IPR cluster on this mesh shows it). I could (1) harden the AFT — test it on multiple synthetic spindle/cone meshes, add the topological repair that removes clusters, and characterize where it fails — giving you an empirically reliable general tool, or (2) return to completing the Thurston construction, which is principled but where I've repeatedly stalled.
I'd argue (1) is the honest path to a general tool you can actually run on your mesh series, with the explicit caveat that it's heuristic and must be validated per-mesh rather than guaranteed. (2) remains the elegant goal but I've shown I can't currently complete it reliably.
Which direction do you want? And to be clear about my own limits: I don't have a method in hand that guarantees a valid fullerene from an arbitrary mesh — the AFT is the closest thing, and it's heuristic. I'd rather tell you that plainly than imply the RSPI handoff is a general solution when it isn't.I don't think the AFT method will work in the general case unless it can deal with the real issue which is "what is the closest true fullerene structure to the mesh derived from experiment".  I think the more principled method is the correct choice, since it should be able to evaluate a numer of close fullerene structures.You've put your finger on the real problem, and stated it more precisely than I had: the goal isn't "produce a valid fullerene from the mesh" — it's "find the closest true fullerene to the experimental mesh, evaluated over a set of nearby candidates." That reframing is decisive, and it's why the principled method is the right choice. Let me explain why I now fully agree, and be honest about what it commits us to.
The AFT produces one triangulation and stops. Even hardened, it can't answer "is there a better-fitting fullerene nearby?" — it has no notion of distance in the space of fullerenes, no way to enumerate or rank alternatives. It's a point estimate with no metric. That's a fundamental limitation, not an engineering gap, exactly as you say.
The Thurston method is the right choice precisely because it provides what the AFT lacks: a metric space of fullerenes. Theorem 0.1 says the valid triangulations are lattice points in ℂ^(1,9), and the mesh's cone metric is a point in that same space (generally not a lattice point). So "closest true fullerene to the mesh" becomes a literal, well-posed question: which lattice points lie nearest the mesh's point in the complex-hyperbolic metric? And "evaluate a number of close structures" becomes: enumerate the lattice points within some radius and rank them. That's not a heuristic — it's a nearest-lattice-point problem in a space where "nearest" has rigorous meaning. The AFT can't even pose that question; Thurston's framework answers it natively.
So the objective is now sharp:

Map the mesh (12 bowls → cone metric) to its point z_mesh in ℂ^(1,9).
Find the lattice points {L_k} near z_mesh (these are the candidate fullerenes — each a valid triangulation by construction, no validity to check).
Rank them by complex-hyperbolic distance to z_mesh (closeness of fit) plus your physical criteria (twist ≤ 30°, IPR, bowl alignment).
The nearest one(s) are the answer; you can inspect the top few.

This is the method you've been pointing toward all along, and it dissolves both objections at once: validity is automatic (lattice points are valid fullerenes), and "closest + evaluate alternatives" is the native operation.

I now understand the error precisely, after five attempts. The gluing condition is wrong: when two triangles share an edge, their copies of the shared vertices should move consistently as positions in 3-space, but on the developed (cut) patch they're related by the edge's developing isometry, not by identity. By demanding identity, I weld the patch rigid. The holonomy lives exactly in this gluing relation — and I keep flattening it to identity, which is why the cone structure (and the signature) vanishes every time.

The Springborn paper (extending Rivin's method, which is what Thurston cites) reveals that Rivin's actual computational approach is not the twisted-cocycle/signature machinery I kept failing at. It's a completely different, and far more robust, formulation:
Rivin's method works with angles as variables, not cocycle coordinates. The key is Theorem 4 (Rivin): a Euclidean cone-metric triangulation with prescribed cone angles exists and is unique (up to scale) iff a "coherent angle system" exists. The variables are the triangle angles, subject to linear constraints:

each angle positive,
the three angles of each triangle sum to π,
the angles around each vertex sum to the cone angle (2π for flat, less for cone points),
(for the Delaunay condition) angles opposite each edge sum to π − θ.

Then the actual metric is found by maximizing a concave function F (built from Milnor's Lobachevsky function) over that linear polytope of angle systems. It's a convex optimization problem with linear constraints — strictly concave (Lemma 2), so the maximum is unique and findable by standard methods. No twisted cocycle, no signature computation, no holonomy gluing — none of the machinery that defeated me five times.
So the honest assessment: I was attacking the wrong formulation. Thurston's ℂ^(1,9) signature picture is the theoretical structure (it tells you the moduli space is complex-hyperbolic), but Rivin's computational method — the one Thurston cites for "various computations" — sidesteps all of it. It reduces "find the cone-metric triangulation" to a concave maximization over a linear polytope, which is exactly the kind of thing that's reliably implementable.
This reframes the whole approach to the closest-fullerene problem:
For a candidate fullerene (a triangulation with 12 cone points of curvature π/3), Rivin's variational principle gives me the actual flat cone-metric realization by convex optimization — robustly, with guaranteed uniqueness. I can then compare that realization to the mesh. The "metric space of fullerenes" and "nearest fullerene" questions become tractable through the variational functional rather than the lattice-signature machinery.
But I want to be careful and honest about scope before promising it works:
What this gives me cleanly: for a given triangulation (combinatorial type + cone angles), compute its canonical flat metric by maximizing the concave F — implementable with scipy, validatable (the maximum is unique). That's a real, robust capability I did not have.
What it does not directly give: the enumeration of which triangulations to consider (the candidate set). Rivin's method realizes a given combinatorial type; it doesn't by itself generate the neighbors. So the "evaluate a number of close fullerenes" still needs a candidate-generation step — but now each candidate can be robustly realized and scored by the variational method, which is the part I couldn't do before.
So here's my honest proposed path, and I'd validate incrementally as before:

Implement Rivin's variational functional (the concave F from Lobachevsky functions) and the coherent-angle-system polytope, and maximize it — validate on the icosahedron (should recover the regular flat cone metric, all angles π/3) and on the 3J3Q triangulation (should recover its metric). This replaces the failed signature step with the method Rivin/Thurston actually used.
If that validates, use it to score candidate fullerenes against the mesh: realize each candidate's metric, compare to the mesh's cone metric, rank.
