Here's the subtlety: for a fullerene triangulation where every vertex is degree 5 or 6, the cone angles are uniform by type (5/6 of triangles... actually every triangle has three corners, and the constraint "angles around a degree-6 vertex sum to 2π" combined with "degree-5 vertex sums to 5π/3" has the all-angles-equal-π/3 solution automatically satisfying every constraint — because a degree-6 vertex gets 6×π/3 = 2π ✓ and degree-5 gets 5×π/3 = 5π/3 ✓). So the regular metric (all equilateral triangles) is always a coherent angle system for any fullerene, and since F is strictly concave with a unique maximum, it's the answer.
What this means, honestly: Rivin's intrinsic cone-metric for any fullerene triangulation is the all-equilateral metric. That's mathematically correct — the intrinsic flat metric with 12 cone points of π/3 is the one where every triangle is equilateral. But it also means the intrinsic metric doesn't distinguish between different fullerene isomers by shape — they all have the same locally-equilateral intrinsic metric, differing only in where the 12 cone points sit combinatorially.
This is actually a clarifying result, not a dead end, but it changes what "closest fullerene to the mesh" must mean:
The intrinsic metrics are all locally-equilateral; what differs between candidate fullerenes is the combinatorial placement of the 12 cone points (which vertices are degree-5). So "closest fullerene to the mesh" is not an intrinsic-metric distance — it's about how well a candidate's cone-point placement, when realized in 3D, matches the mesh's bowl positions and overall shape. The Rivin machinery confirms the intrinsic metric is determined and regular; the discrimination between candidates comes from the embedding (the 3D realization), which is where the mesh's actual geometry enters.
So the validated result is real and useful: I now have a robust, principled method (Rivin's concave maximization, validated on both the icosahedron and the real capsid) that confirms the intrinsic cone metric of any fullerene is the unique all-equilateral one. Your hypothesis is partly right, and the part that's right is useful — but it doesn't break the obstruction the way the test was meant to check.
Splitting it cleanly:
Radial freedom (cone points off the mesh surface): Confirmed correct and useful. Because the fullerene topology depends only on cone-point directions, not radii, a pentamer sitting slightly inside or outside the noisy mesh can't create or destroy defects — but it can sit at its true structural position rather than the noisy apparent one, improving the fit. So your instinct that the noisy mesh shouldn't pin the pentamers exactly to its surface is sound, and it's a freedom worth keeping when scoring fit.

So correcting the cone directions helps the cones themselves but doesn't yield a clean fullerene.

The proof is that the AFT method earlier did produce a clean, defect-free C428 from these same bowls. So a clean closest-fullerene exists; the obstruction in these recent tests is my construction method getting trapped, not the on/off-surface question.

Or is there another angle on the construction method you'd like to try first?just to make sure, can the pentamer bowls  adjust independently in their distance from the original mesh surface?Yes — they can adjust completely independently, and this is worth stating precisely because it's a cleaner and stronger version of the freedom than I conveyed.
Each pentamer's radial distance from the mesh surface is its own free parameter. There's no coupling that forces them to move together — pentamer 1 can sit 10% inside the surface while pentamer 2 sits 5% outside and pentamer 3 sits exactly on it. Twelve independent radial degrees of freedom.
And the reason this is safe to do — why it can't break anything — is the structural fact from the test: the fullerene topology depends only on the cone-point directions, not their radii. So moving any pentamer in or out along its own direction, by any independent amount, leaves the triangulation (which capsomers are pentamers, who connects to whom) completely unchanged. You can slide all 12 independently to wherever they best match the true structure, and you'll still have the exact same valid fullerene — just embedded slightly differently in 3D.
So the picture is: the 12 pentamer directions are what's (approximately) fixed by the bowls and what determines the structure; the 12 pentamer radii are 12 free, independent fit parameters that let each pentamer settle off the noisy surface to wherever it best matches reality.
I should be careful to restate one honest boundary so the independence isn't over-read: this independent radial freedom is purely a fit freedom — it improves how well the cage matches the experimental structure, and each pentamer uses it independently. It does not give independent control over validity — it can't be used to fix defects or change which fullerene you have, because (again) radius doesn't touch topology. That's consistent with the definitive test: the residual-defect problem lives in the construction method, not in where the pentamers sit radially, so independent radial adjustment won't reduce defects, only improve fit.
If it would help make this concrete, I can — once I want to use it — let the relaxer optimize all 12 pentamer radii independently against the mesh envelope when fitting a candidate cage, reporting how far each one ends up off the surface. That would turn this from a stated freedom into an actual per-pentamer fit. Would you like me to build that independent-radii fit into the next concrete cage, or were you mainly confirming the degree of freedom exists?is this true for the rotation of the pentamers around its central axis as well?Yes — and this one is an even cleaner free parameter than the radius, with the same logic but an important nuance.
Each pentamer's rotation about its own central (radial) axis is independent and free, and it doesn't touch the topology at all. The pentamer is a 5-fold symmetric object; spinning it around its axis by some angle doesn't change which capsomers it connects to or that it's a degree-5 disclination. So like the radius, it's a per-pentamer fit parameter — 12 independent rotational degrees of freedom — that you can set to best match the experimental structure without changing the fullerene you have.
But here's the nuance that makes rotation more interesting than radius, and I want to be precise rather than just say "yes":
The radius is a pure fit freedom — it slides the pentamer in/out and genuinely changes nothing about the surrounding lattice. Rotation is subtler, because the pentamer's orientation sets how its 5 edges meet the 5 neighboring hexamers. The disclination (degree-5 vertex) is topologically fixed regardless of spin, so validity is untouched — that part is exactly parallel to the radius. But the in-plane registry between the pentamer's edges and its neighbors is exactly the twist degree of freedom you flagged early in the project (the in-plane shear at capsomer interfaces, the thing you wanted minimized, ≤30°). So pentamer rotation isn't free in the energetic sense the way radius is — rotating a pentamer redistributes twist into its 5 surrounding interfaces.
So the precise answer, separating the senses:
For topology/validity: yes, fully free and independent — rotation can't create or remove defects or change the fullerene, just like radius. Twelve independent rotational degrees of freedom.
For fit: yes, it's a usable per-pentamer fit parameter — you can orient each pentamer to best match the real structure's pentamer orientation.
For energetics/geometry: unlike radius, rotation is not cost-free — it couples to the twist at the 5 pentamer-hexamer interfaces. That's the Ganser/Sundquist cap-geometry and Twarock pseudo-tiling concern from earlier: the real capsid accommodates bounded interface rotations (the ~0–30° twist range), and pentamer orientation is one of the levers that sets where in that range each interface sits.
So in the fit, radius and rotation are both independent per-pentamer freedoms, but they play different roles: radius is a free slide that improves geometric fit at no structural cost, while rotation improves fit and sets the local twist — so you'd want to optimize it jointly with the twist-minimization criterion, not freely, since spinning a pentamer to match one thing may load twist into its five interfaces.
Would you like me, when I next fit a candidate cage, to optimize each pentamer's radius (free) and rotation (against both fit and the twist≤30° criterion) independently — and report the per-pentamer radial offsets and the resulting interface twists?
